Tbh, this game is a great idea, but from what I see here the game needs a critical overhaul to the gameplay loop. As it is sorely lacking in that department, I see why Scott rejected it. I wouldn't trust such a small team to overhaul such a critical aspect of their game in a timely manner. While also avoiding falling apart, and even finding a way to improve it. The concept, ai, and gameplay need a major change. It's very pretty, but if this was finished it would not be good if they stuck to this gameplay loop, it's unsatisfying, unengaging, and unstressful?  Rejecting this was a good call. People talk this up as if the demo was good, it's graphics look good, the movement needs tweaking but also looks serviceable. That is it.

As of typing this, I haven't watched the whole video yet, but Mark is right in that it's a shame that this had to be left unfinished due to issues having FNaF related to it. He's played great similar games like One Night at Flumpty's and Five Nights at Candy's, which of course very similar to and inspired by FNaF. Given people's fascination with this sort of horror, from the experience to the kind of storytelling, there very well could be more of a subgenre of FNaF-likes if people were willing to divorce their ideas from the main FNaF story and send it in their own direction. While the games themselves don't draw me in, trying to read into the lore of FNaF from all the subtle clues hidden throughout is fun, and even the spinoffs have their own intrigue in terms of how they construct their stories.  Heck, FNaF started relatively simple with lots of small pieces of information that was fun to evaluate, only to spin into a complicated web of events as it went on. While none of those FNaF-likes ever got quite that deep, they very well could have if the creators were interested in going wild with their own stories about their own restaurant chains and their murderous animatronics. Here's hoping some of those hopeful developers heed what Mark was saying here and take a FNaF-like experience into it's own rabbit hole of their imagination!

The game seems really cool, the sfx are solid and the visuals & animations are really sleek and fit the atmosphere perfectly. The only thing I'd personally prefer is if the detonation/trap mechanic were part of a stealth aspect of the game, where you can use the line of sight and trap/button positioning to ambush/lead enemies around and manipulate them to their deaths (or stuns, like in the video), and if there was something else for the action and direct confrontation. It felt weird to have such an indirect method of head-on fighting, almost kind of awkward. I just think the awesome speed and mad hops you have should be coupled with some cool action abilities, and maybe the traps and devices could help foster a stealthier side of the game (especially in spookier, closer-quartered settings), but that's just me and I hope they find success with however they decide to move forward with the game. Their dreams may have been crushed, but hopefully new ones can grow to replace them, because there's a lot of potential for a complete game here and they deserve recognition for their hard work. Mark gives REALLY good advice in the intro. FAN WORK /IS/ WORK. it's YOUR work, and you deserve to be compensated for it! Even if the reason you're doing it is reasons besides money, such as for fun, to work on your skill, or to participate in the fandom, the fact remains that you're doing work that benefits others and you deserve reasonable compensation, or at least the ability to own your own product as unique IP. You can change all the names and tell people "oh yeah, it's definitely heavily inspired by (fandom)" and the other fans will still love it ^^ Plus it broadens the market and the creator space and gives new creativity and style into the world!! MAKE YOUR WORK YOUR OWN, you deserve it! Remember, almost every high-fantasy creation after tolkien was a tolkien fanwork, including DnD!!! Anything that has tall, graceful elves, or huge lumbering treefolk or intelligent, body-builder orcs was inspired by tolkien but is recognized as its own thing and rightfully so! (The equivalents of those creatures back in the day were gnomish keebler elves, dryads, and fat, piggy orcs. "Tree Ent" is literally copyrighted by tolkien's family). And do you know how many people publish physical copies of star trek fanfiction???? You can just buy them, as books! Anyway just remember that your work may not be motivated by money, but you still have to eat and pay rent, and people appreciate what you do. Own your shit! Recognize that fanwork is created by YOU, not the fandom creator!

See, something I think Mark is missing with these fangames is that people LOVE to make their own takes and branching universes off of a brand you love. And I can't of course blame Mark at all since I sometimes share his thought process "why are you giving this existing brand your own original concept that you could be better using for your very own project" I get that feeling. However now I realize it's basically the same concept while making fanart, you want to express your love towards the thing you're a fan of, you got a great idea for the thing you enjoy and it's absolutely fine to expand it. I just got a weird feeling since he was pushing the idea of "you can make your own thing it doesn't have to be linked to another existing thing" since that Sonic.EYX game and here again in a more present manner. Once again, not to blame or condemn this way of thinking (since I too share it sometimes) but like I said, there's no shame in doing your fan projects if you feel passionate about it.

I feel like people complaining about ( or mean-spiritedly poking fun at ) Youtubers skipping the tutorials forget that,  1: Information overload is a real issue â€” especially in heavily story-based games â€” and if the first 17 minutes of a game's interactivity is only "WASD is move and space is jump"  then I think it's reasonable that a player would want to actually play a game soon ( especially anyone playing for an audience that may grow impatient, or if the player has only limited time to play games in the first place ), and thus choose to skip to the action if presented the choice. Which leads me directly to... 2:  The dev put a button in the game to skip the tutorial, thus skipping the tutorial is a dev-approved choice and should be accommodated just as much as choices about language settings or mouse sensitivity. I personally do not believe that a player should be punished or insulted for playing a game the way the creator has allowed them to.  I just wanted to get that off my chest, sorry. The thing is, it's perfectly fine to level critisism at a demo! That's the POINT! With a demo, the audience can give critisism about what can be improved upon for the full release of the game! The issue arises when the "critisism" isn't critisism, it's just nitpicky and mean spirited. There's a difference between "The control scheme is a little jank, maybe change the way some of these things work?" and "Your control scheme is dogshit therefore the game is dogshit." This game seems like it could have really used an animation to show you're hanging on to a ledge so people at least know they have the option to pull themselves up, maybe more consistant jumps, either remove fall damage or make it so a certain distance fallen enacts it, and maybe fix the bombs so rather than "line of sight" it's a distance thing + direction + certain materials block it so you COULD still make puzzles out of that  The issue is not critisism, it's giving the creator proper feedback and possible suggestions as to what could have made your experience better.

Watch distractable
The thing is... if someone puts out hot garbage, sometimes they need to know its bad so they can make it better. You're only a bastard for pointing it out if: A) your criticism isn't constructive and B) your criticism is overly aggressive.   If a creator cannot take criticism, they shouldn't post.  Besides, if your goal was to pretend like you like the game... well your true feelings are more than obvious.   That being said, my critique is as follows (If the developers somehow come across this comment): The game has an interesting concept and clearly has had a lot of work put into it. Especially graphically (but I'm no expert in spotting premade assets, so idk).  The biggest drawback is the controls. Second biggest drawback is the AI and game balance. So controls: They need to respond consistently. That is a must. Frankly character movement and interacting with the interface should be pretty solid if you plan to release a demo since, ya know... its what lets you progress through the game. The AI isn't as big of a deal, but its still important. If the monster can't find you when you stand in front of it, that removes the horror. The horror also disappears when escaping the monster is frustrating instead of exhilarating. If defeating the dogbot is important to completing the mission, it needs to be a little more obvious because I didn't pick up on that from this since dogbot never actually went down. Maybe that's because Mark doesn't read tutorials, maybe it was because Mark's webcam covered the current objective. Also the dogbot really shouldn't require more than one interaction. The guy over the radio said to utilize the environment to put the animatronics down for good. A voice line from the protag would be helpful in pointing that out to the player. The robotic arms only hold the dogbot down for a time and the... device... doesn't seem to do much of anything to it. So, in my mind... the way to improve this is to have you run around the junkyard turning on power boxes while evading the dogbot. This emphasizes the threat and gives the player reason to avoid it. Then, the player should have a way to lure the dogbot under a magnetic crane to immobilize it. Retrieve the items from the bot while it can't attack you. Use the crane controls to drop it into a crusher. Boom. you only have to directly interact with the robot once, you give the environment a reason to exist, you prioritize evading the monster to make it more exciting, all in all a better experience by removing some of the tedious gameplay. Also that robot needs to slow sometimes. Make it fast on straightaways and slower to turn. Bob and weave and give the player a dodge roll or something. If he has the 'mad hops' to clear a 30 foot gap, I think he can manage to avoid a charging animatronic. Chase and bite and chase and bite makes for a boring monster. If the game wants to make a point of confronting the animatronics, they need to be interesting to face head to head. Basically, if you want to focus on facing down animatronics, look at movement mechanics in Monster Hunter games as inspiration. Obviously not the player combat, but the monster movements and dodging. If you want to focus on avoiding combat, emphasize stealth mechanics and interacting with the environment to slow down or immobilize the enemy kinda like Amnesia, but not necessarily physics based. The claw thing would be better for escaping the monster than for finishing it off.  Without playing it, I cannot say more for my own review, but its critique like this that can at least get a creator thinking about possible improvements.   You don't have to agree with me, but I do ask you to think it over. This developer knows a lot about their craft and has made something half decent. Its got potential, but it needs significant polish and maybe even some major reworks.   A teacher once told me that there are four 'levels' to how competent you are at a given skill: 1) Unconscious Incompetence - you are not good and you also don't know how bad you are yet. You've just started learning and maybe you've made some things you're proud of, but they don't stand up to any professional work of the same type. 2) Conscious Incompetence - you are not good and you now know how bad you are. Yes this is a good thing and a sign of improvement because, now, you can start to see your own mistakes and learn from them. 3) Conscious Competence - You are good, but you really have to focus on the task to keep that level of skill. You've learned a lot, but there is still a lot of work to go. You make great content but sometimes it shows that you haven't mastered the skill yet. 4) Unconscious Competence - You are good and now you don't even have to focus on the task to do it with great skill. You may or may not be a master, but anyone who sees your work knows you know what you're doing and you'll continue to get better even though you're already pretty damn fantastic.  If you're level 2 or higher, only you know exactly what level you're at. If you're level 1, sometimes it takes someone telling you that you aren't as good as you think you are to push you into level 2.  For all the creators out there still honing their craft, just know you'll get to level 4 if you keep working at it. Accept critique. You don't have to agree with it, but take it into consideration. Don't dwell on it, but consider it. Usually creators are their own biggest critics, so make sure you don't bum yourself out.  Now this is a long-ass comment and if you read to the end, then I appreciate your time investment. Why did I post this here? Mostly because of Mark's resistance to giving any criticism. Its important for learning so don't be afraid to helpfully critique something. Their previous heartbreak shouldn't have any bearing on their project if they still intend to make it. They kept on even though they got denied by Scott. That takes motivation and determination. So if they want to keep at it, you shouldn't baby them and tiptoe around their feelings.

I think I can see why Scott rejected it. Probably had nothing to do with the quality of the project itself. We can all see that the look and mechanics of the game are pretty well done. It's probably the heavy reliance on this made up lore that was an issue to Scott. Not only does it add lore that Scott maybe did not want to have (Aftons voice, the fact that he is still somewhat sane and can be talked with, the current year, how Afton survived etc.), but this game only makes sense with that exact lore.  In order to change the main objective of rebuilding afton, they would have to do a lot of things from scratch. Change models, voicelines, even the storyboard they set up. The developers kind of put themselves into a corner, relying on Scott to approve of their lore for him to accept the game.

Awe, now I see why this game didn't make it. They didn't make afton *Bri'ish*
Quick summary for people who're curious:  Aftonbuilt was made for the Fanverse Initiative. Scott liked the idea but didn't agree to officially fund them and had them continue development on their own for a while. Scott was generally pretty bad at responding during this time and the team was often left waiting for any kind of word back from him. Eventually they were given a deal for funding but not officially made part of the Fanverse, they continued development on the hopes that Scott would make it official. At some point during this there was supposed to be a contract that never got drawn up. Then the whole Scott anti-LGBT incident happened and Scott stepped down, so communications were switched to the new team in charge of the FNaF franchise, who were better at communicating but also strung the Aftonbuilt team along for a while. The Aftonbuilt team had put years into development and hired quite a few people with no guarantee they'd ever be compensated, continuing on nothing but empty promises and a non-existent contract. Eventually, with no real support from Scott or the new FNaF team they cut their losses on what was a huge investment in time and money.

To me, it seems like there is too much in this game, and it takes away from the fun and scariness I think. What I like about FNAF is that you're put right into the middle of the action right off the bat, and of course the tension builds from start to finish. With this one, I think there's too much you have to know and understand before you get into the actual game. There's a tool wheel where you can access all the different tools to use which is cool, but I think for a FNAF game it doesn't appeal to the average user. I think most players will agree they like FNAF because of it's heart, and being such an original arcade horror game at that. Not really because of the complex, in depth mechanics of the game. Actually, now that I think about it, that's just what FNAF was- it was a game with simple mechanics that anyone could understand right off that bat. I guess at the end of the day that's what went wrong here: it became too complex trying to do to much that it overloads the average user with things they need to know, and that takes away from the experience, because now you gotta remember all the mechanics instead of being immersed in the game, making a pretty concept into something kinda generic. All and all, it just tries to do a lot, succeeding at some, but not enough to get a good enough grip on the fun, if you know what I mean. I think that's the main reason Scott didn't support this one through and through. Fun should be prioritized!  I agree with Mark though; if the game wouldn't work as a FNAF game and they're really passionate about the project which I'm sure they are, they could just rebrand it, changing some models, and make it something that's not FNAF, and maybe it could work better that way, appealing to more hardcore gamers!

For those who donâ€™t know, Scott launched the Fazbear Fanverse, which was intended to allow older creators like FNAC and Popgoes to collaborate and sell merch under the fnaf name. Tons of aspiring new creators saw this as an opportunity to pitch their ideas to Scott and have him fund their gameâ€™s development, including Aftonbuilt. Long story short, it took a while, but Scott gave the devs a small portion of the money they asked for, but a couple months later, he backed out on their deal out of fear that the game would be badly received. As terrible as this sounds, this game was developing at the same time as Security Breach, so Scottâ€™s fear after SB launched is not unwarranted. The sad reality of Aftonbuilt is that the creators were not prepared to finish development without Scottâ€™s oversight, and itâ€™s not really what Scott was intending when he launched the Fazbear Fanverse. Itâ€™s not as if the Indie Horror genre belongs to Scott and only Scott, the game can be anything you want it to be and thatâ€™s incredible! Future devs planning on joining Scottâ€™s initiative should consider finishing their games separately first before they consider collaborating with Scott

One person, someone who loves robots, ventures out to these scrap yards to salvage, save, and repair the animatronic pets. Unfortunately, many of them are damaged and react violently -- the "horror" aspect of this game -- but this person is not deterred! They do whatever it takes to get the animatronic under control. Then, they bring the pet back to their shop and repair it, and once it's fixed, it becomes a part of the person's home.   A wholesome horror game.

My Day gets so much better when Mark Uploads a Video
Aftonbuilt feels like it got caught in the fallout of Security Breach. According to interviews with the devs, they were kept waiting for months at a time by Scott, waiting for info, or confirmation of a contract, funding, etc. Aside from some funding here and there, Scott was largely silent, and when the time came to get a contract signed to make the entire game, Scott pulled the plug, calling it too much of a risk.  This was shortly after the release of Security Breach, and the fallout that came after. It's rather evident that after Security Breach, Scott didn't trust ambitious projects-- after all, if his team couldn't do it, how could an indie team? So the years of work that went into building this demo from the ground up were effectively wasted, which is a damn shame, because Jeliliam was one of the best fan game devs we had, and seeing his first real interactions with Scott go this poorly is a damn shame. This game and its developers very clearly had a LOT of potential, and it would fill in a very big "???" section of the FNAF timeline (the time between 3 and Pizzeria Sim), but Scott just isn't ready to put his resources into a game of this scale after Security Breach.  It's an even BIGGER shame, because so far this rough, somewhat broken, and EXTREMELY incomplete demo has gotten high praise from everyone who's played it.

The experiment was such a success that they made a show as a fun little cover up. The lead scientist was Dr.Afton or better known as "Purple Guy" or as the kids know him, the "lean monster".
